<p>Examination of-Figure Two will reveal that the primary
        distinction made within the system of classificatory verbs is
        that between animate and inanimate. Inanimate objects are
        further categorized into liquids and non-liquids, and the
        non-liquids in turn into two categories: objects which are
        either long and rigid or flexible, and objects which are not
        long and rigid nor flexible. The following list contains some of
        the classificatory verbs alphabetized by the form used for the</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 304</p>
      <p>animate category. The related forms are listed under each entry
        in the list. It should be noted that the individual stem forms
        of each classificatory verb are treated as distinct verbs in the
        dictionary; i.e., for each classificatory verb there are five
        separate entries.</p>
      <table>

          <tr>
            <td colspan="3" rowspan="1" style="vertical-align: top;
              text-align: center;">List of Classificatory Verbs</td>
          </tr>

      <p>I-F.<a name="I-F"></a> <u>The verb "to be."</u> The verb "to
        be" in Cherokee is irregular. It appears as i²³gi in the present
        tense, as ge¹sv²³ʔi in the past, and as ge¹se³sdi in the future.
        The present tense form i²³gi rarely occurs except when preceded
        by the relative prefix j- or by the negative prefix y- (see <a
          href="#I-A">I-A</a> for a discussion of these prefixes). The
        verb "to be" is not required in Cherokee in sentences like (1).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮎ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"The man is big." <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>na</td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>u²³tạ²na</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>the</td>
            <td>man</td>
            <td>big.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The present tense form of "to be," i²³gi, generally functions
        as a bearer of the prefixes j- and y-, as in (2-3).</p>

      <p>Sentences such as (4) are not commonly used in Cherokee; they
        tend to occur in formal styles, such as the style employed in
        writing obituaries.</p>
      <p>The past tense form of "to be" is ge¹sv²³ʔi, consisting of the
        stem ges- followed by the suffix -vʔi. ge¹sv²³ʔi may be used
        with a noun or adjective to indicate past time, as in (5).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

        comparative sentence in (13).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(13) <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏂ <br>

            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎨᏒᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"John is bigger than
              Charley."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>ja²³ni <br>
            </td>
            <td>u¹go³²di <br>
            </td>
            <td>u¹tạ²na <br>
            </td>
            <td>si²³hnv <br>
            </td>
            <td>ja²³li <br>
            </td>
            <td>ge¹sv⁴ʔi. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>John</td>
            <td>more</td>
            <td>big</td>
            <td>than</td>
            <td>Charley</td>
            <td>is</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The past tense form of "to be" is always employed in
        comparative sentences like (13). Note that the comparative
        ge¹sv⁴ʔi is the same as the past tense ge¹sv²³ʔi except for a
        difference in pitch.</p>
      <p>A complete analysis of "to be" has not been attempted and
        further study of this highly important irregular verb is needed.</p>
      <p align="center">Section II.<a name="SectionII"></a> The Noun</p>
      <p>The prefixes and suffixes which can occur with nouns in
        Cherokee are discussed in <a href="#II-A">II-A</a> and <a
          href="#II-B">II-B</a>. Section <a href="#II-C">II-C</a> deals
        with the possessed nouns, nouns which always refer explicitly to
        a possessor. Section <a href="#II-D">II-D</a> is a brief
        discussion of compound nouns, and Section <a href="#II-E">II-E</a>
        lists some of the more important kinds of nouns derived from
        verbs in Cherokee.</p>
      <p><u>II-A.<a name="II-A"></a> Noun prefixes</u></p>
      <p><u>di-, plural</u></p>
      <p>form: di- before a following consonant, j- before a vowel, d-
        before -u- in some non-possessed nouns; cf. (1-3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᏁᏌᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>kạ²ne¹sạ³ʔi</td>
            <td>"box"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎧᏁᏌᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>dị²kạ²ne²sạ³ʔi</td>
            <td>"boxes"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏪᏥ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²we²³ji </td>
            <td>"egg"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏧᏬᏥ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jụ²we<sup>22</sup>ji</td>
            <td>"eggs"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(3)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏓᏁᎲᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u¹dạ²ne²hv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"puddle"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏚᏓᏁᎲᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>du¹dạ²ne²hv⁴ʔi&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"puddles"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>function: di- marks the plural of some inanimate nouns in
        Cherokee. Many nouns referring to inanimate objects do not mark
        the plural, however; compare (4-5).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᏁᏌᎢ ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>kạ²ne¹sạ³ʔi jị²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td>"I see a box."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎧᏁᏌᎢ ᏕᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>dị²kạ²ne¹sạ³ʔi de²³jị³gowh¹tị³ha.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I see boxes."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(5)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎫᎫ ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gu¹gu jị²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td>"I see a bottle."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td colspan="5">
              <hr color="darkgray">
              <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 307</p>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎫᎫ ᏕᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>gu¹gu de²³jị³gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td>"I see bottles."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Each inanimate noun which takes di- in the plural is given in
        its plural as well as its singular form in the dictionary. Nouns
        for which no plural form is given are not inflected for
        plurality.</p>
      <p>comment: di- is the same prefix which appears as de- in verb
        forms. Compare the verb form in (6) with the noun derived from
        it in (7).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(6) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᎬᏃᏌᏍᎦ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>de²³gv³no²sạ³sga.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"He's sweeping them."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(7) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎬᏃᏌᏍᎩ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>dị²gv²no²³sạ²sgi.</td>
            <td>"sweeper (of plural objects)"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>a-, human</u></p>
      <p>form: a-.</p>
      <p>function: Nouns referring to persons exhibit a- in the third
        person, as in (8-10).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(8) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya <br>
            </td>
            <td>"man"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(9) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎨᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ge²³hya&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"woman"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(10) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏧᏣ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²chu⁴ja&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"boy"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>comment: Some nouns referring to persons take a- optionally in
        the singular, although a- must appear in plural forms of the
        same noun; note (11).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(11) <br>
            </td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎦᎾᎦᏘ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gạʔ³nạ²kti</td>
            <td>"doctor"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎾᎦᏘ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạʔ³nạ²kti</td>
            <td>"doctor"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>c. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᎦᎾᎦᏘ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ni²gạʔ³nạ²kti&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"doctors"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>-n-, plural</u></p>
      <p>form: -n- before a vowel, -ni- before a consonant.</p>
      <p>function: -n- marks the plural in the case of animate nouns, as
        in (12); it also indicates plurality of the possessor in forms
        such as (13).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(12) <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ni²sgạ²ya&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(13) <br>
            </td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏪᏥ<br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²we²³ji</td>
            <td>"his offspring"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>
            <td>ᎤᏁᏥ <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²ne²³ji</td>
            <td>"their offspring"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>Prefixes employed in non-third person forms</u>. Nouns
        referring to human beings are inflected for person and number by
        the use of the Set A prefixes used with a large class of
        Cherokee verbs (see Section <a href="#I-B">I-B</a> for
        discussion and examples. Paradigm One includes the first,
        second, and third person forms of "man" in the singular, dual,
        and plural.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm One</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>1. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ji¹sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"I - man"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>2.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>hị²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"you - man"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>3.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"man"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>4.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏂᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ni²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"you and I - men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>5.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏗᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹di²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"you (pl) and I - men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>6.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏍᏗᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²sdi²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"he and I - men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td colspan="4">
              <hr>
              <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 308</p>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>7.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏥᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>

            <td>o²ji²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"they and I - men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>8.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏗᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>

            <td>sdi²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"you (dl) - men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>9.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏥᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ji²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"you (pl) - men"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>10. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ni²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>"men"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Noun forms referring to human beings may be used as complete
        sentences, as in (14)</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(14) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏍᎦᏯ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>ji¹sgạ²ya. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I'm a man."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The predicative use of form (14) is shared with other nouns in
        Cherokee; note (15) which could answer the question "what is
        it?"</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(15) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᏁᏌᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>kạ²ne¹sạ³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"It's a box."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Second person human forms may be used as terms of address, as
        in (16).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(16) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏏᏲ ᎯᏧᏣ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>sị²yo hị²chu⁴ja.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Hello, boy."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Inanimate nouns may take the same prefixes shown in Paradigm
        One to form surnames. Note Paradigm Two, in which the noun a³ma
        "salt" takes the Set A prefixes to create the surname "Salt."
        Note that a glottal stop is inserted between the prefix and the
        initial -a- of a³ma in each form.</p>

      <p>II-B.<a name="II-B"></a> <u>Noun suffixes</u></p>
      <p><u>-i, locative</u></p>
      <p>form: -i.</p>
      <p>function: The locative suffix carries the meaning "place of. "
        It is added to nouns in Cherokee to form place names, as in
        (1-4).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎫᏌ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gu⁴sa</td>
            <td>"Creek"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎫᏐᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gu²so⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"Muskogee" (i.e. "Creek place")</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᏟ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gi²hli</td>
            <td>"dog"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏟᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gi²hli⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"dog place"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(3)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᎨᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ni²ge²³hya</td>
            <td>"women"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᎨᏴᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ni²ge²hyv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"place of women"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td colspan="5">
              <hr color="darkgray">
              <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 309</p>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎪᎦ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ko³ga</td>
            <td>"crow"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎪᎬᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ko³gv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"crow place"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>comment: When -i is added to a noun ending in -a, the -a
        changes to -o- in some nouns, and to -v- in others; note (1),
        (3), and (4).</p>
      <p><u>-hi "on, in"</u><br>
        form: -hi.</p>
      <p>function: This suffix means "on" or "in," cf (5-7). It can also
        mean "into"; as in (8).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(5) <br>
            </td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏄᎸ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²nu²³lv</td>
            <td>"grass"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏄᎸᎯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²nu²lv⁴hi&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"in the grass"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(6)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏅᎹ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a³ma</td>
            <td>"salt"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎼᎯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a²mo⁴hi</td>
            <td>"in salt"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(7)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏍᎩᎶ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga²sgị²lo</td>
            <td>"table"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏍᎩᎸᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga²sgị²lv⁴ʔi&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"on the table"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(8)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏔᎷᏣ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>tạ²lu²³ja</td>
            <td>"basket"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏔᎷᏦᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>tạ²lu²jo⁴hi</td>
            <td>"into the basket"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>comment: -hi requires a change of final -a to -o- in (6b) and
        (8b). In (7b) the suffix takes the form -i rather than -hi,
        requiring a change in the final vowel of the noun form -o to
        -v-. Further study is needed to determine whether more than one
        suffix is involved and precisely what kinds of final vowel
        changes are caused by the addition of the suffix or suffixes
        illustrated by (5-8b).</p>
      <p><u>-ha "all, only"</u></p>
      <p>form: -ha.</p>
      <p>function: -ha is used with the meaning "only" as in (9-10), and
        with the partitive meaning, as in (11).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(9) <br>
            </td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏲᏁᎦ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>yo³ne²ga gạ²wo³nị²ha.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"He's speaking English."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>

            </td>

            </td>
            <td>"He's speaking only English."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(10) <br>

      <p><u>-yaʔi "pure, real"</u></p>

        "pure" or "real," as in (14-15).</p>

      <p><u>Suffixes which also occur with verbs.</u> A number of
        suffixes occur with both nouns and verbs. These include -gwu,
        -sgo, -na, -ju, -ke, -hv, and -dv. Since each of these suffixes
        has been described in <a href="#I-D">I-D</a> above, they will
        not be discussed in detail, but simply illustrated in noun
        forms.</p>
      <table>

      <table align="center" border="1">
        <caption>Figure 1</caption> <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>
              <p>-i, locative</p>
              <p>-hi "on, in"</p>

            </td>

      <p>II-C.<a name="II-C"></a> <u>Possessed nouns</u></p>
      <p>A number of nouns in Cherokee always occur with a possessive
        prefix. Kinship terms as well as terms for body parts and some
        articles of clothing fall into this</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 311</p>
      <p>category. It is impossible, for example, to say simply "hand"
        in Cherokee unless a detached hand or an image of a hand is
        understood; it is necessary to say "my hand, your hand," etc.
        Discussion and illustrative paradigms follow.</p>
      <p><u>Body parts.</u> Some terms for body parts indicate the
        possessor by the use of the Set A pronoun prefixes used with
        verbs, while others take the Set B prefixes (see Section <a
          href="#I-B">I-B</a> for discussion). The use of the Set A and
        Set B prefixes with body part terms is illustrated by Paradigms
        One and Two, respectively.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm One: "nose"</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>1.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏴᏐᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ji¹yv²so⁴li</td>
            <td>"my nose"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>2.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᏓᏐᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td>hị²hyv²so⁴li</td>
            <td>"your nose"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>3.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᏴᏐᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td>kạ²yv²so⁴li</td>
            <td>"his nose"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>4.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏂᏴᏐᎵ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>di¹ni²hyv²so⁴li</td>
            <td>"your and my noses"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>5.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏗᏴᏐᎵ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>di¹di²hyv²so⁴li</td>
            <td>"your (pl) and my noses"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>

            <td>"his and my noses"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>

            <td>jo²ji²hyv²so⁴li</td>
            <td>"their and my noses"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>

      <p>Paradigms One and Two illustrate the formation of plurals of
        single body parts in cases in which two or more individuals are
        involved. Compare Paradigm Three, which illustrates how plurals
        are formed in the case of paired body parts.</p>

      <p><u>A note on detached body parts.</u> Terms for detached body
        parts differ from the corresponding non-detached forms with
        respect to the noun stem as well as by the absence or presence
        of the possessive prefix; cf. (1-2).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎦᏔ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²kta</td>
            <td>"eye" (detached)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎦᏙᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td>a²kto⁴li <br>
            </td>
            <td>"his eye"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that possession of detached body parts is indicated by the
        use of the possessive ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi "my," as is the case with
        other non-possessed nouns; cf. (3-4)</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(3) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᏁᏌᎢ ᎠᏆᏤᎵ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>kạ²ne²sạ³ʔi ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴i <br>
            </td>
            <td>"my box"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎦᏔ ᎠᏆᏤᎵ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²kta ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"my eye"(detached)</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The eye referred to in (4) could be understood to have been
        detached from the speaker's body, from someone else's body, or
        to be a picture or some other representation of an eye.</p>
      <p>It was pointed out in Section <a href="#II-A">II-A</a> that
        the plural prefix appears as de- before a following consonant in
        verb forms and as di- before a consonant in nouns. There are a
        few exceptions to this, including the plural forms for "toe" and
        "finger"; note (5-6).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(5)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᎾᏌᏛᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>kạ²na³²sạʔ²dv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his toe"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᎧᎾᏌᏛᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>de²³kạ²na³²sạʔ²dv⁴ʔi&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"his toes"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(6)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏰᏌᏛᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga³ye²sạʔ³dv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his finger"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>

            <td>de²³ga³²ye²sạʔ³dv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his fingers"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p> <u>A list of body part terms.</u> A list of some of the more
        frequently used terms for body parts in Cherokee follows. Terms
        for detached as well as non-detached, possessed forms are
        included; note that some separate terms for detached body parts
        are not as common as others and do not always exist.</p>

      <p><u>Kinship terms.</u> Nouns indicating family relationships are
        inherently possessed. The same set of prefixes used with
        transitive verbs such as "to like" (see Section <a href="#I-B">I-B</a>)
        is used with kin terms, as illustrated by Paradigm Four.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm Four: "father"</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>

      <p>Compare Paradigm Five, the paradigm of "child," which includes
        forms which do not occur in the paradigm of "father." For
        example, there is no form in the paradigm of "father" which
        corresponds to (35) in Paradigm Five, since a single person
        never has more than one father, except in a non-literal sense
        which seems strange to Cherokee speakers.</p>

      <p align="center">Notes on Paradigms Four and Five</p>
      <p>In Paradigm Four, as noted above, forms which do not ordinarily
        occur have not been included. An example is "you (dl) are my
        fathers" which is somewhat unnatural for Cherokee speakers since
        an individual can have only one natural father. In Paradigm Five
        all possible forms are included in the paradigm, although (4),
        (15), (17) and (19) are somewhat odd since these forms involve
        situations in which a single individual is the child of more
        than two parents, once again forcing a non-literal
        interpretation. These forms have been included in Paradigm Five,
        however, so that the full range of forms is displayed in at
        least one entire paradigm.</p>
      <p>Note that the stem of "child" begins with a vowel, while that
        of "father" begins with a consonant. As a result, the prefixes
        indicating possession change in form in exactly the same way in
        which they change in verb forms. See Section <a href="#I-B">I-B</a>
        for more detailed discussion of the changes in form which the
        pronoun prefixes undergo depending on whether the following stem
        begins with a vowel or with a consonant.</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 317</p>
      <p><u>Non-singular kinship terms.</u> Some kinship terms, as well
        as a few other nouns referring to persons, do not exhibit
        singular forms. A noun of this type is "friend," shown in
        Paradigm Six.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm Six: "friend"</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>1. <br>
            </td>

            </td>
            <td>gị²na²li⁴ʔi </td>
            <td>"you and I - friends"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>2.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᎦᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ga²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you (pl) and I - friends"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>3.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎩᎾᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²gị²na²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"he and I - friends"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>4.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎩᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²ga²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"they and I - friends"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>5.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏓᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>sda²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you (dl) - friends"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>6.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏣᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ja²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you (pl) - friends"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>7.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎾᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²na²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"they - friends"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The forms in Paradigm Six can be used as the subjects of plural
        verbs, as one would expect; cf. (7-8).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(7) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏓᎵᎢ ᏍᏗᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>sda²li⁴ʔi sdi²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"You (dl) friends are speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(8)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᎾᎵᎢ ᎢᏂᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gị²na²li⁴ʔi i¹ni²wo³nị²ha.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"You and I, friends, are speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Since there are no forms of "friend" in the singular, it is
        necessary to utilize the forms in Paradigm Six to express
        meanings such as "my friend" and "your friend." Note (9-15), in
        which the forms from Paradigm Six are given their singular
        meanings.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(9)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎩᎾᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²gị²na²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"my friend"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(10) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏓᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>sda²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"your friend"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(11)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎾᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²na²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his friend"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(12)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᎦᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ga²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"your (sg or pl) and my friend"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(13)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎦᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²ga²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his and my, their and my friend"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(14)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏣᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ja²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"your (dl,pl) friend"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(15)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎾᎵᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²na²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"their friend"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>When a non-singular noun like "friend" occurs as the subject of
        a sentence in its singular sense, it requires a singular verb
        form; cf. (16-17). In addition, the verb form must exhibit the
        third person singular prefix.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(16) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎩᎾᎵᎢ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²gị²na²li⁴ʔi gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"My friend is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(17)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎾᎵᎢ ᎠᎵᏍᏓᏴᎲᏍᎦ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²na²li⁴ʔi al¹sda³²yv²hṿ³sga.</td>
            <td>"His friend is eating."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>

        of some of the more important Cherokee kinship terms.</p>

            <td>"his son (adult)"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><a name="II-D">II-D</a>. <u>Compound nouns</u></p>
      <p>The formation of compound nouns is somewhat limited in
        Cherokee. The compound in (1c) is grammatical, while that of
        (2c) is not; the grammatical equivalent of (2c) is (3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎪᏢᏅ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>go²hlv²nv</td>
            <td>"butter"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎵᏙᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gahl³doh²di</td>
            <td>"container"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>c.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎪᏢᏅ ᎦᎵᏙᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>go²hlv²nv gahl³doh²di</td>
            <td>"butter container"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏩᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>wạ²hya</td>
            <td>"wolf"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᎠᏯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya </td>
            <td>"man"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>c.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><span style="color: red;"><font
                  color="red">*ᏩᏯ ᎠᏍᎦᏯ</font></span> <br>
            </td>
            <td><font color="red">*wạ²hya ạ²sgạ²ya</font></td>
            <td>"wolf man"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(3)</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏩᏯ ᎢᏳᏍᏗ ᎠᎦᏍᎦ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>wạ²hya ị²yu⁴sdi ạ²sgạ²ya&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"wolf-like man"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Productive patterns do exist for the formation of compound
        nouns. A transitive verb form may be changed to a noun by the
        nominalizing suffix -i (see Section <a href="#II-E">II-E</a>);
        when such verb forms have a direct object, the nominalization
        results in a compound noun; note (4).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏥᎳ ᎨᏠᎭ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ji²³la ge²hlọ³ha.</td>
            <td>"He's feeding the fire."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td> <br>
            </td>
            <td>b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏥᎳ ᎨᏠᎭ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ji²³la ge²³hlọ²ha.</td>
            <td>"fire-feeder"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Similarly, an intransitive verb form with a subject may be
        nominalized, yielding a compound like (5).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(5)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏔᎦ ᎠᏂᏴᎯᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²ta²³ga a¹ni²yv³hị³ha.</td>
            <td>"Chickens are going in."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td colspan="5">
              <hr color="darkgray">
              <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 319</p>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td> <br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏔᎦ ᎤᏂᏴᏍᏗ Ꭲ <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²ta²³ga u²ni²yv²sdi⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"where chickens go in" (i.e., chicken house)</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that forms like u²ni²yv²sdi⁴ʔi are formed by adding the
        locative suffix to the infinitive suffix -di.</p>
      <p>Compound nouns like (4b) and (5b) can then be used as subjects
        or objects of sentences, as in (6-7).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(6) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏥᎳ ᎨᏠᎯ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ji²³la ge²³hlọ²hi gạ²wo²nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"A Catholic is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(7)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏔᎦ ᎤᏂᏴᏍᏗᎢ ᎠᎩᏑᏫᏒᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²ta²³ga u²ni²yv²sdi⁴ʔi a¹ksu²³hwị³sv²³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"I painted the chicken house."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><a name="II-E">II-E</a>. <u>Derived nouns</u></p>
      <p>There are a number of processes in Cherokee by which nouns may
        be derived from verbs. Several types of noun derivation are
        commented on briefly in this section.</p>
      <p><u>Agentive forms.</u> Agentive nouns, which can frequently be
        translated by the -er suffix in English, are formed by adding -i
        to the verb stem, as in the example (1b).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎪᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³ni²sgo³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He speaks (habitually)."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎩ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo¹ni⁴sgi</td>
            <td>"speaker"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note the additional examples in (2-3) .</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏍᏚ ᏓᏲᎯᎰᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ji²sdu da¹yo²hị²ho³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He shoots rabbits (habitually)."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏍᏚ ᏗᏲᎯᎯ<br>
            </td>
            <td>ji²sdu di²yo²³hị²hi</td>
            <td>"rabbit shooter"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(3)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎯᏏᏙᎴᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³ni²hị²si²³do³²lẹ²ha.</td>
            <td>"He's going around speaking for him."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᎯᏏᏗᎴᎯ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²wo¹ni²³hị³si²do¹le⁴hi</td>
            <td>"one who goes around making speeches for him"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>There are situations which require noun forms with -i rather
        than the corresponding verb form. An example is (5), which is
        derived from (4). While (5) can be understood to have an animate
        or inanimate subject, (4) must have an animate subject.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏗᏍᎪᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a¹di²³sgọ³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He says it," or "He says (habitually)."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏗᏍᎩ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²di⁴sgi</td>
            <td>"one that says"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The derived form in (4b) would have to be used, for example, in
        (5), in which an inanimate object is said to "say" something.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(5) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᎠ ᏥᎪᏪᎳ "Ꭽ" ᎠᏗᏍᎩ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>hị²ʔa jị²go²hwe⁴la "ha" ạ²di⁴sgi.</td>
            <td>"This letter says "ha."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The sentence in (6) can only be understood to have an animate
        subject.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(6) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"Ꭽ" ᎠᏗᏍᎪᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"ha" a¹di²³sgọ³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He says 'ha' (habitually)."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>Nouns derived from infinitive verb forms</u>. Nouns which
        refer to the action or state of the verb may be formed from
        infinitive forms, as in (7).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(7) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏚᎵ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He wants him to speak."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td>u²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi</td>
            <td>u¹du²li.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>him to speak</td>
            <td>he wants</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"speech"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Derived nouns like (7b) require the use of the third person
        singular prefix used in present tense forms of the relevant
        verb, and the infinitive suffix -di. Not</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 320</p>
      <p>all verbs permit the formation of derived nouns such as (7b);
        an unabridged dictionary would include derived forms like
        "speech" as separate entries.</p>
      <p>The infinitive suffix may be used with the Set B prefixes to
        form derived nouns referring to one's facility in performing the
        action of the verb, as in (8-10).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(8)&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎦᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gị²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi <br>
            </td>
            <td>"my facility in speaking"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(9)&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td>jạ²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi</td>
            <td>"your facility in speaking"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(10) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏂᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²ni²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi</td>
            <td>"their facility in speaking"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>(7b) might be used to refer to a class in speech, while (8-10)
        can only be understood to refer to an individual's facility in
        speaking.</p>
      <p>Cherokee infinitive forms may be modified in pitch to express
        obligation or necessity; compare the a-forms in (11-13) with the
        b-forms.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(11)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi u¹du²lị³ha.</td>
            <td>"He wants to speak."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi.</td>
            <td>"He must speak."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(12)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎪᏩᏛᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²gowh²tvh³di u¹du²lị³ha.</td>
            <td>"He wants to see it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎪᏩᏛᏗ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²gowh²³tvh²di.</td>
            <td>"He must see it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(13)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏧᎸᏫᏍᏓᏁᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ju²lv²³hwị³sdanh¹di u¹du²lị³ha.</td>
            <td>"He wants to work."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏩᏙᎵᏫᏍᏓᏁᏗ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ju²lv²³hwị²sdanh¹di.</td>
            <td>"He has to work."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The b-forms in (11-13) function as verbs, but this construction
        is discussed here because of its similarity to the nouns derived
        from infinitives. Note that forms like (7b) and (9) can serve as
        subjects and direct objects of sentences, as in (14-15).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(14) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ ᎠᎩᎸᏉᏗ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi a¹gị²lv²³kwdi. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I like speech."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(15)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ ᎣᏍᏓ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jạ²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi o⁴sda.</td>
            <td>"Your ability to speak is good."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>On the other hand, forms like (11b) serve as predicates in
        sentences such as (16).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(16) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮎ ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᎤᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>na³ ạ²sgạ²ya u²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"That man must speak."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Instrumental nouns may also be derived from infinitive forms by
        using the third person singular prefix for the verb in question
        and replacing the infinitive suffix by the instrumental -dohdi;
        cf. (17-19).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(17)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi u¹du²lị³ha.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"He wants him to speak."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎯᏍᏙᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo¹ni²³hị²sdoh²di</td>
            <td>"instrument to speak with"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(18)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏛᎪᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²tv²³go³²di u¹du²lị³ha.</td>
            <td>"He wants to hear it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏛᎪᏙᏗ&nbsp; <br>

            <td>"instrument to hear with"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(19)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎬᏴᏂᏍᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gv²yv²ni¹sdi u¹du²lị³ha. </td>
            <td>"He wants me to hit you."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎬᏅᏆᎶᏍᏙᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gv²nv²gwạ²lo³sdoh²di</td>
            <td>"instrument for me to hammer you with"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The infinitive suffix may be used with the third singular
        prefix used in present progressive forms to form derived nouns
        with the meaning "something to be ___ "; note (20).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(20)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎩᏍᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>

            </td>

            <td>"something to be eaten"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 321</p>
      <p>Note that the type of derived noun illustrated by (20b) differs
        from the type illustrated by (7b); cf. (21-22).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(21)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>

            <td>gạ²wo¹ni²³hị³sdi </td>
            <td>"speech"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>

            <td>"something to be spoken" (i.e., a speech to be made)</td>

          <tr>
            <td>(22)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏅᏆᎶᏍᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>

            <td>u²nv²gwạ²lo¹sdi u¹du²lị³ha.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"He wants to hammer."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏅᏆᎶᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²nv²gwạ²lo⁴sdi</td>
            <td>"something to be hammered, hammer"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>In the case of a few lexical items, forms like (22b) are used
        to refer to the instrument itself. The Cherokee term for
        "hammer" is thus (22b) rather than the expected instrumental
        form (23), formed on the same pattern as (17-19b).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(23) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏅᏆᎶᏍᏙᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²nv²gwạ²lo³sdoh²di&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"instrument to hammer with"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>(23) is thus understood to refer to any kind of an instrument
        for hammering including the conventional hammer.</p>
      <p>The infinitive suffix is also used together with the prefixes
        illustrated below in Paradigm One to indicate ability or
        capacity to perform the action of the verb.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm One: "to speak"</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎬᎩᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gv²gị²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"I am able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏣᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga²jạ²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"you are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎬᏩᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gv²wa²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"he is able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎩᏂᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga²gị²ni²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"you and I are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏲᎩᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²yo²gi²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"they and I are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏲᎩᏂᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²yo²gi²ni²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"he and I are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏍᏗᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga²sdi²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"you (dl) are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏥᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga¹ji²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"you (pl) are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏩᏂᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gv²wạ²ni²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi</td>
            <td>"they are able to speak"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The forms contained in Paradigm One are verb forms, but we have
        included this construction in this section because of its
        similarity to the derived nouns under discussion. Note that the
        forms in Paradigm One can only be understood to refer to the
        ability of a person to speak in present time. (24), for example,
        might be used as a reply to the question "Is he able to speak?"
        when inquiring about a person who has lost his voice.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(24) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꭵ, ᎬᏩᏬᏂᎯᏍᏗᏛ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>v, gv²wạ²wo¹ni²³hị²sdi³dv.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Yes, he is able to speak."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Forms such as (8-20), on the other hand, are derived nouns and
        refer to the facility a person has at performing the action of
        the verb rather than to the physical or mental ability to do so.</p>
      <p><u>Nouns derived from past tense verb forms</u>. Derived nouns
        which may be translated by an -ing verb form in English may be
        formed in Cherokee from past progressive forms by means of a
        shift in pitch, as in (25-27).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(25)&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎬᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³ni²sgv²³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He was speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎥᎢ<br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³ni²sgv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his speaking"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(26)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᏍᎬᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a¹gowh²ti²³sgv³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He was seeing it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td colspan="5">
              <hr color="darkgray">
              <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 322</p>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᏍᎬᎢ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a¹gowh²ti²³sgv⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his seeing it"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(27)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏕᎵᏍᎬᎢ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>tsde²li²³sgv³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He was helping you."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏕᎵᏍᎬᎢ. <br>
            </td>

            <td>"his helping you"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The b-forms in (25-27) are understood to refer to specific
        acts; (25b), for example, might be used in a sentence such as
        "His speaking yesterday was not clear."</p>
      <p>Derived nouns like (28b) may be formed from past tense forms
        like (28a).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(28)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎯᏴᎢ.&nbsp; <br>

            <td>"He left it behind."</td>
          </tr>

            </td>

            <td>"something left behind"</td>
          </tr>

      <p><a name="II-F">II-F</a>. <u>First person singular forms of
          nouns</u></p>
      <p>It was noted in Section <a href="#I-A">I-A</a> that all nouns,
        including those referring to inanimate objects, can be
        personified by the use of the subject pronoun prefixes, as in
        (1). See also <a href="#II-A">II-A</a>, Paradigm Two.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎹ <br>
            </td>
            <td>a³ma</td>
            <td>"salt"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎠᎹ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ʔa³ma</td>
            <td>"Salt"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>c.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᎠᎹ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²ʔa³ma.</td>
            <td>"I'm a Salt."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Personified forms of inanimate nouns like "salt," which begin
        with a vowel, are formed by the addition of the pronoun prefixes
        displayed in <a href="#II-A">II-A</a>, Paradigm Two, with a
        glottal stop inserted in each instance between the prefix and
        the initial vowel of the inanimate noun. Note also that the
        prefix vowel which occurs directly before the glottal stop is
        short. The same set of prefixes are used with inanimate nouns
        beginning with a consonant, except that no glottal stop is
        inserted after the prefix; cf. (2a-c).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏂᏍᏓ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gan²sda</td>
            <td>"stick"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎦᏂᏍᏓ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gan²sda&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Stick"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>c.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᎦᏂᏍᏓ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²gan²sda&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I'm a Stick."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>These nouns form the first person singular by replacing the
        third person prefix inherent in the noun.</p>
      <p>The following list includes those nouns which do not form
        non-third person forms as in (1-2) above. For each of these
        nouns, the third person and first person singular forms are
        shown. Given the first person singular form, the first person
        dual and plural and the second person forms of each verb can be
        predicted. For example, if a first person form has the subject
        pronoun prefix ji-, the second person singular form will have
        the prefix hi-, etc., as in (3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(3)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏬᏂᏍᎩ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²wo¹ni⁴sgi.</td>
            <td>"I'm a speaker."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᏬᏂᏍᎩ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>hị²wo¹ni⁴sgi.</td>
            <td>"You're a speaker."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>c.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏗᏬᏂᏍᎩ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>sdi²wo¹ni⁴sgi.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"You (dl) are speakers."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>If the first person singular form of a given noun has g- as the
        prefix, then the second person singular form will have h-, etc.,
        as in (4).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎬᏃᏌᏍᎩ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>gv²no³sạ²sgi.</td>
            <td>"I'm a sweeper."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>

            </td>

            <td>"You (dl) are sweepers."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 323</p>

        an- before noun stems beginning with a vowel, and of ani- before
        noun stems beginning with a consonant, as in (5-6).</p>

      <p align="center"> <a name="SectionIII">Section III</a>. Other
        Parts of Speech</p>
      <p><a name="III-A">III-A</a>. Pronouns</p>
      <p><u>Personal pronouns</u></p>
      <p><span style="text-decoration: underline;">ᎠᏴ </span><u>ayv "I,
          we"</u></p>
      <p>This pronoun has a variant, ạ²ya, which is used by some
        speakers. It refers to one or more first persons. It is
        frequently used in replying to questions; note (2) as a response
        to the question in (1).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎪ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏪᏅᏎᎢ? <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"Who went?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td>ga³go</td>
            <td>u¹we²nv³se³ʔi?</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>who</td>
            <td>went</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <br>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(2) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏴ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²yv.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>ᎠᏴ ạ²yv is also used in stative sentences like (3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(3) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏴ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏣᎳᎩ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I'm a Cherokee."&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²yv <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²jạ²lạ²gi</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>I</td>
            <td>Cherokee</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>ᎠᏴ ạ²yv can also be used for emphasis as the subject of a verb.
        This use of is optional, as (4) indicates.</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 325</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"I am speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏴ ᏥᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²yv jị²wo³nị²ha.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I am speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᏂᎯ nihi "you"</u></p>
      <p>nị²hi is used in addressing one or more second persons. It
        functions like ạ²yv, as illustrated by (5-8).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(5) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᎪ
              ᏄᏛᏁᎴᎢ?&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ga³go nu¹dv³ne²le³ʔi?</td>
            <td>"Who did it?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(6) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏂᎯ.&nbsp;





              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">nị²hi.</td>
            <td>"You."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <br>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(7) <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᏲᏁᎦ.&nbsp;







              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"You're a white man."</td>
          </tr>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp;







              <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"You're speaking."</td>
          </tr>

            </td>
