        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>ᎠᎬ ạ²yv and ᏂᎯ nị²hi are the only independent personal pronouns
        in Cherokee. The demonstrative pronouns are employed in place of
        third person independent pronouns, as noted below.</p>
      <p><u>Demonstrative pronouns</u></p>
      <p>The demonstrative adjectives discussed in Section <a
          href="#III-B">III-B</a> can stand alone as demonstrative
        pronouns, as in (9-10)</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(9)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᎠ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏆᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp;








              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I want this."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hị²ʔa</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹gwạ²du²lị³ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">this</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">I want</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(10) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎨᏍᎩᎾ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He sees that one." <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">v¹sgị²na</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">that one</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he sees</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>These demonstrative pronouns may refer to human beings when the
        context makes it clear that a human is being referred to, as in
        (11-13).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(11) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᎠ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I see him."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hị²ʔa</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ji²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(12)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎥᏍᎩᎾ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I see them (an)."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">v¹sgị²na</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">&nbsp;gạ²ji²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">them</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">I see</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(13)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᎠ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hị²ʔa</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³nị²ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>In (11) the verb form indicates an animate direct object;
        compare (14-15).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(14) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>ji²gowh¹tị³ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I see him."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(15)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²gowh¹tị³ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I see it."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Since it is clear from the verb form used in (11) that a person
        is being seen, ᎯᎠ hị²ʔa is understood to mean "him." Similarly,
        the form of the verb used in (12) indicates that there is an
        animate plural direct object. hị²ʔa can thus mean "he, him, she,
        her, they, them" when referring to animate nouns which are near
        the speaker. ᎥᏍᎩᎾ v¹sgị²na has the same English translations
        when referring to animate nouns which are some distance from the
        speaker.</p>
      <p><u>Reflexive pronouns.</u></p>
      <p>The reflexive pronouns are more complex than the personal and
        demonstrative pronouns. They are inflected in a manner similar
        to verbs. Paradigm One contains the reflexive pronoun forms
        "myself, yourself," etc.</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 326</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm One</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏋᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gwv²³sa</td>
            <td>"myself"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏨᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>jv²³sa</td>
            <td>"yourself"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏩᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²wa²³sa</td>
            <td>"himself, itself"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᏅᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>gị²nv²³sa</td>
            <td>"ourselves" (you sg. and I)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᎬᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹gv²³sa</td>
            <td>"ourselves" (you pl. and I)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎩᏅᏌ&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²gị²nv²³sa <br>
            </td>
            <td>"ourselves" (he and I)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎬᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²gv²³sa</td>
            <td>"ourselves" (they and I)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏛᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>sdv²³sa</td>
            <td>"yourselves" (you dl.)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏨᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹jv²³sa</td>
            <td>"yourselves" (you pl.)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏅᏌ <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²nv²³sa</td>
            <td>"themselves"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The stem of the reflexive pronoun is -vsa; -v- changes to -a-
        in the third person singular form ᎤᏩᏌ ụ²wa²³sa. For discussion
        and analysis of the pronoun used in Paradigm One, see Section <a
          href="#I-B">I-B</a>. Sentences (16-17) illustrate the use of
        the above forms.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(16) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏋᏌ ᎦᏓᎪᏩᏘᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gwv²³sa gạ²da²gowh¹tị³ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"I see myself."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(17)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏩᏌ ᎠᏓᏛᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²wa²³sa a¹da²dv²hnị³ha.</td>
            <td>"He's hitting himself."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note the reflexive pronouns are redundant, and do not have to
        be used in sentences like (16-17) since the presence of the
        reflexive prefix in the verb form makes the reflexive meaning
        clear; compare (18-19).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(18) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏓᎪᏩᏔᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²da²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td>"I see myself."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(19)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏓᏛᏂᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>a¹da²dv²hnị³ha</td>
            <td>"He's hitting himself."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Reflexive pronouns often stand alone in sentences like (21),
        said in reply to questions like (20).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(20) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎪ ᎯᎪᏩᏘᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga³go hi²gowh¹tị³ha?</td>
            <td>"Who do you see?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(21)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏋᏌ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gwv²³sa.</td>
            <td>"Myself."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The dual and plural reflexive pronouns may never be used with
        verb forms understood reciprocally. While verbs with the
        reflexive prefix -adad- and the plural prefix de- can ordinarily
        be interpreted as either plural reflexives or reciprocally,
        sentences like (22-23) can only have the plural reflexive
        meaning because of the presence of the reflexive pronouns. See
        the discussion of de- and -adad- in Section <a href="#I-A">I-A</a>
        for further details of forms which can be interpreted either as
        plural reflexives or reciprocally.</p>
      <table style="width: 877px;">
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="width: 34px;">(22) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; width: 146px;">ᎤᏅᏌ ᏓᏅᏓᎪᏩᏘᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="width: 238px;">u²nv²³sa da²nạ²da²gowh²tị³ha.</td>
            <td style="width: 433px;">"They see themselves (plural acts
              )."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="width: 34px;">(23)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; width: 146px;">ᎣᎬᏌ ᏙᏣᏓᎪᏩᏘᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="width: 238px;">o²gv²³sa do¹jạ²da²gowh²tị³ha.</td>
            <td style="width: 433px;">"They and I see ourselves (plural
              acts)."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>There is a second set of reflexive forms identical to those of
        Paradigm One except in pitch. These reflexives, displayed in
        Paradigm Two, have the meaning "I alone" or "only I," etc.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm Two</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>

      <p><u>Indefinite pronouns</u></p>
      <p><u>ᎩᎶ&nbsp; </u><u>kilo "somebody, someone"</u></p>
      <p>kilo is illustrated by (25).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(25) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᎶ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>kị²lo gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"Someone is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>Ꮭ ᎩᎶ&nbsp; hla kilo "no one, nobody"</u></p>
      <p>This pronoun is formed by combining the negative hla "not" and
        kị²lo as in (26).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(26) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮭ ᎩᎶ ᏱᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>hla³ kị²lo yị²gạ²wo³²nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"No one is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᎪᎱᏍᏗ&nbsp; gohusdi "something"</u></p>
      <p>This form has several variants, including gohusd, gosd, and
        gusd. The use of a particular form depends upon the speaker and
        upon the formality of the speech style.</p>
      <p><u>Ꮭ ᎪᎱᏍᏗ&nbsp; hla gohusdi "nothing"</u></p>
      <p>The structure of this pronoun parallels that of Ꮭ ᎩᎶ&nbsp; hla³
        kị²lo.</p>
      <p><u>ᏂᎦᏗ&nbsp; nigada "all, everyone, everything"</u></p>
      <p>This pronoun may stand alone or may serve adjectively to modify
        nouns; its use is illustrated by (27-28).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(27) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎦᏓ ᎠᏆᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>nị²ga⁴da a¹gwạ²du²lị³ha</td>
            <td>"I want all of it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(28)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎦᏓ ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ ᎠᏂᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>nị²ga⁴da ạ²ni²sgạ²ya a¹ni²gowh²tị³ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"All the men see it."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>Interrogative pronouns</u></p>
      <p><u>ᎦᎪ&nbsp; gago "who"</u></p>
      <p>This pronoun refers only to persons; note (29).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(29) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎪ ᎯᎪᎡ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga³go hi²gọ²ʔe³?</td>
            <td>"Who did you see?"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᎦᎩ&nbsp; gagi "who"</u></p>
      <p>This pronoun refers to persons and is used in situations in
        which the speaker has not understood the identity of a person
        just referred to by someone else; note (30-31).</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 328</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(30) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏂ ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎬᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>ja²³ni gạ²wo³ni²sgv²³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"John was speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(31)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎩ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>ga³²gi? <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Who?" (i.e. "Who did you say?")</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᎦᏙ&nbsp; gado "what"</u></p>
      <p>The use of this question word calls for an answer containing a
        verb; i.e., the question refers to the nature of the action
        being performed by the person spoken to. An example is (32),
        which might be answered by (33).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(32) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎯᏛᏁᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²do² hạ²dv³nẹ²ha?</td>
            <td>"What are you doing?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(33)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎪᏪᎵ ᏕᏥᎪᎵᏰᎠ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>dị²go²hwe²li de²³jị³go²li²³yẹ³ʔa </td>
            <td>"I'm reading a book."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ&nbsp; gado usdi "what"</u></p>
      <p>This question word is a compound consisting of ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ gạ²do²
        and u²sdi. It calls for a noun answer. The question in (34)
        might be answered by (35).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(34) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: middle;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ ᎯᎪᏩᏘᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">gạ²do<sup>2 </sup>u²sdi
              hị²gowh²tị³ha?</td>
            <td>"What do you see?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(35)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: middle;">ᎤᏔᎾ ᏗᏆᎴᎳ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²³tạ²na dạ²gwạ²le³la.</td>
            <td>"A big car."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>gạ²do² u²sdi can also be used to question the subject of the
        sentence, as in (36), which could be answered by (37).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(36) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ ᎾᏛᏁᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²do² u²sdi na¹dv³nẹ²ha?</td>
            <td>"What's doing it?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(37)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏃᎴ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²no²le.</td>
            <td>"The wind."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ&nbsp; gado usdi "which"</u></p>
      <p>This pronoun differs from ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ gạ²do<sup>2 </sup>u²sdi
        "what" only in pitch. Its use is illustrated by (38-39).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(38) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ ᏣᏚᎵᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²do² u⁴sdi jạ²du²lị³ha?</td>
            <td>"Which one do you want?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(39)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ ᎾᏛᏁᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²do² u⁴sdi na¹dv³nẹ²ha? <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Which one is doing it?"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ&nbsp; gado usdi can also modify nouns, as in (40).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(40) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: middle;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²do<sup>2 </sup>u⁴sdi ạ²sgạ²ya gạ²wo³nị²ha?</td>
            <td>"Which man is speaking?"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>Possessive pronouns </u></p>
      <p>The stem of the possessive pronoun is -jeliʔi. This stem is
        employed to form the possessive pronouns displayed in Paradigm
        Three.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm Three </u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎪᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"mine"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏆᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>di²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"mine" (pl)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>jạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"yours"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏣᏤᎵᎢ <br>

            <td>"yours" (pl)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏤᎵᎢ <br>

            <td>u²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏧᏤᎵᎢ <br>

            <td>ju²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"his" (pl)</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᎾᏤᎵᎢ <br>

            <td>gị²nạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"ours" (your sg. and mine)</td>

            <td>"ours" (your pl. and mine, pl.)</td>

              <hr color="darkgray">
              <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 329</p>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᎩᎾᏤᎵᎢ <br>

            <td>"ours" (his and mine)</td>
          </tr>

            <td>"yours (dl)"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏍᏓᏤᎵᎢ <br>

            <td>"yours (dl)" (pl)</td>

            <td>"yours (pl)"</td>

          </tr>
          <tr>

            <td>"theirs" (pl)</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The plural prefix occurs with the possessive forms in instances
        in which there are two or more possessed items. This prefix
        appears as di- before a following consonant and as j- before a
        following vowel.</p>
      <p>The use of the possessive pronouns is illustrated by (41-42).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(41) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: middle;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏍᏗ ᎠᏆᏤᎵᎢ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²do<sup>2 </sup>u⁴sdi ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi?</td>
            <td>"Which one is mine?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(42)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᎠ ᏧᏤᎵᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>hi²ʔa ju²je²li⁴ʔi.</td>
            <td>"These are his (pl)."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The personal pronouns may be used together with the possessive
        pronouns, as in (43-44).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(43) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏴ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏆᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏍᏓ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"Mine is good." <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²yv</td>
            <td>ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>o⁴sda.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>I</td>
            <td>mine</td>
            <td>good</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(44) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏆᏚᎵᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I want yours." <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>nị²hi <br>
            </td>
            <td>jạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>a¹gwạ²du²lị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>you</td>
            <td>yours</td>
            <td>I want</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The possessive pronouns may also function as adjectives, as in
        (45-46).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(45)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏓᏆᎴᎳ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᎦᎨᎸ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"Your car is red." <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>dạ²gwạ²le³la <br>
            </td>
            <td>jạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>car</td>
            <td>yours</td>
            <td>red</td>
          </tr>

            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(46)</td>

            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏆᏤᎵᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"My car is big."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>dạ²gwạ²le³la</td>
            <td>ạ²gwạ²je²li⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>u²³tạ²na.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>car</td>
            <td>mine</td>
            <td>big</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>Pronoun suffixes </u></p>
      <p>The pronouns can occur with the suffixes -gwu, -sgo, -ju, -ke,
        -hv, -dv, and -na, described in <a href="#II-B">II-B</a>.
        Examples follow.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(47) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏴᏊ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²yṿ³gwu</td>
            <td>"just me"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(48)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎯᏍ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>nị²his³?</td>
            <td>"Is it you?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(49)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏤᎵᏧ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²je²li⁴ju?</td>
            <td>"Well, is it his?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(50)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏂᏍ, ᏂᎯᎨ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>ja²³nis³, nị²hị³ke?</td>
            <td>"Is it John or you?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(51)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎯᏍᎪᎲ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>nị²hị³sgo¹hv?</td>
            <td>"But is it you?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(52)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꭵ, ᏂᎯᏛ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>v, nị²hị²dv.</td>
            <td>"Yes, it's you."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(53)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎯᎾ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>ni²hi⁴na?</td>
            <td>"And what about you?"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>See the discussion of each of these suffixes in <a
          href="#II-B">II-B</a>.</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 330</p>
      <p><a name="III-B">III-B</a>. Adjectives</p>
      <p>Adjectives in Cherokee generally precede the nouns they modify.
        This fact has provided a criterion for identifying adjectives.
        In (1) the adjective precedes the noun; in (2) two nouns form a
        compound noun construction.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏚᎯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᏍᏆ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏃᎯᎵ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A pretty bird is flying."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²wo²du⁴hi</td>
            <td>ji³sgwa</td>
            <td>gạ²no²hi²³li.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>pretty</td>
            <td>bird</td>
            <td>is flying</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏔᎦ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏨᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏴᎦ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A rooster is crowing."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²ta²³ga</td>
            <td>ạ²chv²ya</td>
            <td>ayh³ga.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>chicken</td>
            <td>male is</td>
            <td>crowing</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>A semantic criterion has also been used to classify words as
        adjectives: those forms which are understood to impute a quality
        to a person or object are classified as adjectives, even though
        the noun referring to that entity may not always appear in the
        sentence. An example is (4), the response to (3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(3) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯᏍ ᎯᎪᏩᏘᎭ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²yas³ hi²gowh¹tị³ha? <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Do you see a man?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꭵ, ᎤᏔᎾ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>v, u²³tạ²na.</td>
            <td>"Yes, a big one."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note also that adjectives can function as adverbs modifying
        verbs. This fact provides a second criterion for identifying
        adjectives. For example, u²³tạ²na can serve as an adverb, as in
        (5).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(5)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²³tạ²na gạ²wo³nị²ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"He's talking big."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Adjectives differ from verbs with respect to affixation. The
        initial prefixes and the suffixes which occur with verbs in
        Cherokee do not occur with adjectives. Compare (6-7); in (6),
        the past tense suffix -vʔi is attached directly to the verb form
        while it cannot be attached to the adjective in (7).
        Instead,-vʔi is attached to the past tense form of the verb "to
        be" in (7).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(6) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎬᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³ni²sgv²³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He was speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(7)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾ ᎨᏒᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²³tạ²na ge¹sv²³ʔi.</td>
            <td>"He was big."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Adjectives are inflected for person and number by the use of
        the subject pronoun prefixes described in Section <a
          href="#I-B">I-B</a>. Paradigm One illustrates the use of the
        Set A prefixes with adjective stems beginning with a consonant.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm One: "red"</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>1.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>jị²gi²gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>2.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>hị²gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>3.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"he - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>4.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>gi²gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"it - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>5.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏂᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ni²gi²gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you and I - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>6.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏗᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹di²gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you (pl) and I - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>7.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏍᏗᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²sdi²gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"he and I - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>8.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏥᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²ji²gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"they and - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>9.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏗᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>sdi²gi²³gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you (dl) - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>10. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏥᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹ji²gi²gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"you (pl) - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>11.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ni²gi²gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"they (an) - red"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>12.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎩᎦᎨᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td>di²gi²gạ³ge⁴ʔi</td>
            <td>"they (inan) - red"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 331</p>
      <p>Note that the plural object prefix is used to form the plural
        of inanimates, as in (12); this prefix takes the form di- in
        adjectives, just as it does in nouns.</p>
      <p>Paradigm two illustrates the use of the Set A prefixes with an
        adjective stem beginning with a vowel.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm Two: " good"</u></p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎪᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>go³²sda</td>
            <td>"I - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎰᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ho⁴sda</td>
            <td>"you - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o⁴sda</td>
            <td>"he, it - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏃᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹no⁴sda</td>
            <td>"you and I - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏙᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹do⁴sda</td>
            <td>"you (pl) and I - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏍᏙᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²sdo⁴sda</td>
            <td>"he and I - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏦᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²jo⁴sda</td>
            <td>"they and I - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏍᏙᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>sdo⁴sda</td>
            <td>"you (dl) - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏦᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>i¹jo⁴sda</td>
            <td>"you (pl) - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏃᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²no⁴sda</td>
            <td>"they (an) - good"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏦᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td>jo⁴sda</td>
            <td>"they (in) - good"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that adjectives like "red," which begin with consonants,
        show a contrast between third person singular inanimate and
        third person singular animate forms. This contrast is not
        present in the case of "good" and other adjective stems like it
        which begin with stem initial vowels. Paradigm Three displays an
        adjective stem beginning with a consonant which requires the Set
        B prefixes.</p>
      <p><u>Paradigm Three: "crazy"</u></p>

      <p>The adjectives in Paradigms Four and Five have stems beginning
        with vowels and require the Set B prefixes; note that the
        initial -a- of the stem is dropped in third person forms in
        Paradigm Five. Note the insertion of -w- between the third
        person prefix u- and the initial stem vowel -o- in Paradigm
        Four.</p>

      <p><u>Third person plural adjective forms.</u> Third person plural
        inanimate adjective forms are marked for plurality by the
        addition of the plural prefix, as noted above; this prefix takes
        the form di- before a following consonant, and j- before a
        following vowel. Third person plural animate forms of verbs
        which take the Set B prefixes require a double marking; the j-
        prefix followed by -u- followed by -n-.</p>
      <p><u>Categorization of fruits for purposes of adjective
          inflection.</u> Terms for fruits in Cherokee require the same
        form of a modifying adjective as animate nouns do. Note (8-10).</p>

      <p><u>The demonstrative adjectives.</u> The demonstrative ᎯᎠ hị²ʔa
        means "this, these." It modifies both animate and inanimate
        nouns; note (11-13).</p>

      <p>ᎥᏍᎩᎾ v¹sgị²na or its variant forms ᎥᏍᎩ v¹sgi and ᏍᎩᎾ sgị²na
        "that, those," is used to refer to an object or person some
        distance from the speaker, as in (14-15).</p>

      <p>ᎥᏍᎩᎾ v¹sgi²na is used to modify nouns referring to objects
        which are out of reach of the speaker but are visible, as in
        (16).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(16) <br>
            </td>

            </td>
            <td>v¹sgị²na ạ²sgạ²ya gạ²wo³nị²ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td>"That man is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>ᎾᏍᎩ na¹sgi is used more or less interchangably with ᎥᏍᎩ v¹sgi.
        ᎾᏍᎩ na¹sgi seems to function as a marker of formal or biblical
        style.</p>
      <p>The demonstrative adjectives also function as pronouns, as
        noted in Section <a href="#III-B">III-B</a>. na is a noun
        modifier which functions much like the definite article "the" in
        English; note (17-18 ).</p>
      <table>

      <p><u>The terms for the ordinal numbers.</u> The terms for the
        ordinal numbers are formed by the addition of the ordinal suffix
        to the terms for the cardinal numbers. This ordinal suffix
        appears as -ine when preceded by final vowels -a and -i as in
        2-10, 22-30, 32-40, etc. (the final vowel sound is deleted
        before -ine); as -sine when the cardinal number term ends in -u
        as in 11-19, and as -hine in the terms for 21, 31, 41, 51, etc.
        The term for "first," ị²gv²yi⁴, is irregular.</p>
      <p>Ordinal terms may serve as the subject or object of sentences
        when the modified noun is deleted, provided that the auxiliary
        "to be" is employed with the relative prefix j-; note (22).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

      <p><u>Quantifying adjectives</u>. Quantifying adjectives are words
        like "all," "a few," and "many." Note the following examples.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(23) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏂᎦᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏬᏂᎭ.&nbsp;








              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"All the men are speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">nị²ga⁴da</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ni²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹niwo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">all</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">men</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">are speaking</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(24) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏲᏟ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏗᎦᏁᏌᎢ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏓᏆᏚᎵᎭ.&nbsp;








              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I want a few boxes."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>

            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">dị²gạ²ne¹sạ³ʔi <br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">da¹gwạ²du²lị³ha. <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏧᏣ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ.&nbsp;








              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I see many boys."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">tsgwi⁴sdi</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ni²chu⁴ja</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²ji²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">many</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">boys</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">I see </td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The quantifiers illustrated above can stand alone, as in

      <p><u>Adjective suffixes</u>. Several of the suffixes which have
        been discussed above in sections dealing with the verb and the
        noun may also be attached to adjectives. These include -gwu,
        -sgo, -ju, -ke, and -dv. Examples follow.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(29)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏍᏗᏊ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²sdi⁴gwu</td>
            <td>"just little"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(30)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾᏍ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²³tạ²nas³?</td>
            <td>"Is it big?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(31)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎣᏍᏓᏧ? <br>
            </td>
            <td>o²³sdạ²ju?</td>
            <td>"Well, is it good?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(32)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾᏍ, ᎤᏍᏗᎨ?&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>u²³tạ²nas³, u²sdi⁴ke? <br>
            </td>
            <td>"Is it big or little?"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(33)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꭵ, ᎤᏔᎾᏛ.&nbsp; <br>
            </td>
            <td>v², u²³tạ²nạ²dv.</td>
            <td>"Yes, it's big."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>These suffixes are described in Section <a href="#I-C">I-C</a>
        and in Section <a href="#II-B">II-B</a>. Some additional
        suffixes occur only with adjectives, and several of these will
        be discussed briefly.</p>
      <p>Some adjectives take the suffix -jv, "very"; note (34).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(34) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏧᏃᏚᏨ <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏥᎸᏍᎩ
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏓᏛᎯᏍᏗᏍᎪᎢ.





              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He grows very beautiful
              flowers."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>

            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">da¹tṿ²hị²sdi²³sgọ³ʔi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">very beautiful <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">flowers</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he grows </td>

      <p>The suffix -igeʔi may be translated by the English suffix
        "-er"; note (36).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

      <p>-igeʔi causes a preceding -a to delete. When added to an
        adjective ending in -i, the form of the prefix is -geʔi; note
        (37).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(37)</td>

            </td>
            <td>"faster"</td>

      <p>The function of -igeʔi is illustrated by (38).</p>
      <table>

      <p>Some adjectives form the comparative by adding the suffix -ya.
        These same adjectives form the superlative by adding -yvʔi; note
        (39).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(39)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>

            </td>
            <td>"smartest"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The prefix w- is used together with -yvʔi to form the
        superlative.</p>

        w-; note (40).</p>

      <p>Adjectives like "pretty" exhibit a change in the final
        consonant of the stem from -h- to -s- before the superlative
        suffix -vʔi. The final suffix of "pretty" may be dropped; note
        also that the final -vʔi suffix of superlative adjective forms
        may also be dropped.</p>
      <p><a name="III-C">III-C</a>. Adverbs</p>
      <p>In this section the function of adverbs in Cherokee is
        illustrated. Adverbs may modify verbs, adjectives, or other
        adverbs, as in (1-3) respectively.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᏓᏯ </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏚᎸᏫᏍᏓᏁᎭ.





            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He's working hard."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sda⁴ya <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">du¹lv²³hwị³sda¹nẹ²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hard</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he's working</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <br>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2) </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏙᎯᏳ </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏔᎾ </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮎ </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ. </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"That man is really big."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>u²do²hị²yu</td>
            <td>u²³tạ²na</td>
            <td>na³</td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>really</td>
            <td>big</td>
            <td>that</td>
            <td>man</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <br>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

      <p>The negative word hla "not" fulfills an important adverbial

      <table>

          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(4) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">Ꮭ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎣᏍᏓ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏱᎨᏎᎢ. <br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">yị²ge³se³ʔi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>

      <p>The above sentences illustrate the relative order in which
        several adverbs occur together in a single sentence. Note that
        sentence adverbs, which include in their domain the entire
        sentence, can occur in more positions than adverbs which serve
        to modify the verb alone. For example, adverbs like "tomorrow"
        are sentence adverbs because they establish the time reference
        for the entire sentence, as in (36), while adverbs like "hard"
        refer specifically to the action of the verb in sentences like
        (37).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>

      <p>In the dictionary, a number of adjectives which frequently
        serve as adverbs are designated as both adjective and adverb.
        Words which are labeled only as adjectives may generally also
        function as adverbs. For example, the adjective gi²³gạ²ge⁴ʔi
        "red" may modify verbs like "paint," as in (41).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

              <br>

          </tr>

            </td>

            </td>

        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that the numeral adjectives, including the terms for the
        ordinal numbers, do not function as adverbs. Similarly, the
        demonstrative adjectives cannot function as adverbs. Some of the
        quantifying adjectives, including "many" and "few," can function
        adverbially, as in (42-43); others, like "all," cannot.</p>

      <p><a name="III-D">III-D</a>. Prepositions</p>
      <p>There are relatively few words in Cherokee which function like
        prepositions in English. Those which do function like the
        English prepositions are, strictly speaking, postpositions
        rather than prepositions, since they follow the nouns which
        serve as their objects. The traditional English term preposition
        is retained here, however, in order not to confuse the reader
        unfamiliar with linguistic terminology.</p>
      <p>The preposition ᎬᏗ gvh⁴di "with" occurs very frequently in
        Cherokee. Its use is illustrated by (1-2).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏅᏆᏕᏍᏗ
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎬᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He's hitting it with a
              hammer."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²nv²gwạ²lo⁴sdi <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gvh⁴di</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gv²hnị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hammer</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">with</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he's hitting</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏲᏁᎦ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎬᏗ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He's speaking in English."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yo³ne²ga</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gvh⁴di</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">English</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">with</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he is speaking</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that the objects of prepositional phrases with ᎬᏗ gvh⁴di
        refer to an instrument, as in (1), or to something by means of
        which the action of the verb is carried out. In (2), for
        example, the subject of the sentence is said to speak by means
        of English.</p>
      <p>The preposition di³dla "toward" is used with motion verbs, as
        in (3-4).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(3) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᎵᏦᏕ <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏗᏜ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏩᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He's walking toward the
              house."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gahl²jo²³de</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">di³dla</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">wa³ʔi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">house</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">toward</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he's walking</td>

            <td style="text-align: center;">near</td>

          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The prepositions in (6-9) are formed with ᏗᏜ di³dla, but
        function as single lexical items.</p>
      <p>It should be noted that a number of relationships expressed in
        English and other languages by prepositions are expressed by
        different means in Cherokee. There is no preposition in
        Cherokee, for example, equivalent to the English preposition
        "at." In Cherokee the locative noun suffix fulfills the same
        function. A second example is the dative-benefactive suffix,
        which is the equivalent of the English preposition "for."
        Additional examples could easily be found. Prepositions in
        Cherokee are relatively few in number, apparently, because the
        rich variety of prefixes and suffixes employed in this language
        fulfills many of the functions of prepositions in other
        languages.</p>
      <p><a name="III-E">III-E</a>. Interjections</p>
      <p>Those Cherokee vocabulary items which can occur alone as
        exclamatory utterances are classified as interjections. The
        greeting ᎣᏏᏲ o²sị³yo or ᏏᏲ&nbsp; sị²yo "hello" is perhaps the
        most important interjection in Cherokee. Note that the terms for
        leave-taking in Cherokee are delayed imperative verb forms; e.g.
        (1).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏙᎾᏓᎪᎲᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>do³nạ²da²go²hv²ʔi.</td>
            <td>"Let us (you and I) see each other again later."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᎬᎪᎥᎢ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>i²³gv³gọ²ʔv²ʔi.</td>
            <td>"Let me see you again later."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note the additional interjections in (3-10).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(3) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏲ! <br>
            </td>
            <td>ayo!</td>
            <td>"Ouch!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(4)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ᏺ!<br>
            </td>
            <td>yo!</td>
            <td>"Gosh!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(5)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꭽ!<br>
            </td>
            <td>ha!</td>
            <td>"Gosh!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(6)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮒ!<br>
            </td>
            <td>ni!</td>
            <td>"Look!" or "Listen!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(7)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮎ!<br>
            </td>
            <td>na!</td>
            <td>"Here!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(8)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮟ!<br>
            </td>
            <td>si!</td>
            <td>"Wait!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(9)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎧᎹ! <br>
            </td>
            <td>kam!</td>
            <td>"Now!"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(10)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏄᎳ! <br>
            </td>
            <td>nu⁴la!</td>
            <td>"Hurry!"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><a name="III-F">III-F</a>. Conjunctions.</p>
      <p>Conjunctions join words and clauses together. This section
        includes examples of several of the more important conjunctions
        in Cherokee.</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 343</p>
      <p><u>ᎠᎴ ale "and"</u></p>
      <p>This conjunction is used in relatively formal speech styles in
        Cherokee and in written style. It is illustrated by (1-3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎴ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎨᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏬᏂᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man and a woman are
              speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²le</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ge²³hya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹ni²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">man</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">and</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">woman</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">are speaking</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎴ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏕᎧᏃᎩᎠ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man is speaking and
              singing.</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³nị²ha <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²le</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">de²³kạ³no²gi³ʔa.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">man</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is speaking</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">and</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is singing</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(3)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏧᏣ <br>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎴ <br>

      </table>
      <p>As the above examples show, ᎠᎴ ạ²le can be used to connect
        nouns and verbs; it can also join sentences. In most spoken
        styles, is not used, however; the casual equivalents of (1-3)
        are (4-6).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(4)&nbsp; </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎨᏯᏃ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏬᏂᎭ.
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man and a woman are
              speaking." </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sgạ²ya <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ge²³hyạ³hno <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹ni²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">man</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">woman and</td>

            </td>

          </tr>

            <td><br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">de²³kạ³no²gi³ʔạ³hno.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">man</td>

          </tr>

      </table>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

          </tr>

            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³nị²ha <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ge²³hyạ³hno <br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">de²³kạ³no²gi³ʔa.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">boy</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is speaking</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">woman and</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is singing</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that the suffix -hno is attached to the first word of the
        second clause in (6) .</p>
      <p><u>-Ꮄ ᏱᎩ -le yigi "or"</u></p>
      <p>The suffix -le is used together with the negative form of "to
        be," yi⁴gi, to express the meaning "or"; note (7).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(7)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ ᎠᎨᏯᎴ ᎭᎩ. <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya gạ²wo³nị²ha ạ²ge²³hyạ³le yi⁴gi.</td>
            <td>"A man or a woman is speaking."</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><u>ᎥᏍᏊ Ꮭ vsgwu hla "neither, isn't either"</u></p>
      <p>The adverb ᎥᏍᏊ v¹sgwu "also" is used with the conjunctive
        suffix -hno and the negative word hla to express the meaning
        "neither" or "isn't either"; note (8).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(8) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>

      <p><u>ᎢᏳᏍᏗ iyusdi "because"</u></p>
      <p>ᎢᏳᏍᏗ ị²yu⁴sdi can fulfill an adverbial function; it can also
        function as a conjunction, as in (14).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(14) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏫᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏚᏯᏪᎦ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏚᎸᏫᏍᏓᏁᎲ
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎢᏳᏍᏗ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"Bill is tired because he
              is working."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">wi²³li <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">du¹yạ²we³ga <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">du¹lv²³hwị³sda¹ne²hv⁴ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ị²yu⁴sdi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">Bill</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is tired</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he's working</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">because</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that the suffix -ᎮᏃ -he²³hno, or its short form -Ꮓ -hno,
        also means "because," as in (15).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(15) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏚᏯᏪᎦ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏚᎸᏫᏍᏓᏁᎭᎮᏃ.





              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He's tired because he's
              working."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">du¹yạ²we³ga <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">du¹lv²³hwị³sda¹ne²hạ³he²³hno.





              <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he is tired</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he is working because</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>It is clear that ᎢᏳᏍᏗ ị²yu⁴sdi is not a suffix because the
        preceding verb can be questioned, as in (16).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(16) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ᏚᎸᏫᏍᏓᏁᎲᎢᏍ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎢᏳᏍᏗ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏚᏯᏪᎦᏉ?
            </td>
            <td>"Is he tired because he's working?</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">du¹lv²³hwị³sda¹ne²hv⁴ʔis²








              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ị²yu⁴sdi





              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">du¹yạ²we³ga?</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Similar questions cannot be formed with -ᎮᏃ -he²³hno, which
        seems to function as a suffix.</p>
      <p>See <a href="#IV-C">IV-C</a> for further discussion of
        conjunction in Cherokee.</p>
      <p align="center"><a name="SectionIV">Section IV</a>. Some Notes
        on Cherokee Syntax</p>
      <p>The purpose of this section is to provide some information on
        Cherokee syntax. Various grammatical processes are discussed
        briefly and a number of sentence types are commented on in the
        sections which follow.</p>
      <p><a name="IV-A">IV-A</a>. <u>Negative sentences</u></p>
      <p>Declarative sentences such as (1a) can be made negative by the
        use of hla and the negative verb prefix (see Section <a
          href="#I-A">I-A</a>).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1)</td>
            <td>a.</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ᏓᎻ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ᎬᏂᎭ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ᏫᎵ.</td>
            <td>"Bill is hitting Tom."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">da²³mi
              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">gv²hnị³ha





              <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">wi²³li.
              <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">Tom</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is hitting</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">Bill</td>
          </tr>

      <p>Sentences with adjectives as predicates in Cherokee may be

        tense; note (2-3).</p>

            <td style="text-align: center;">Tom and</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">also</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">not</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note (19), in which the verb of the main clause is negated,
        while the direct object of the main clause is also the subject
        of a relative clause.</p>

        different in structure from their Cherokee equivalents. Note
        (26-27), for example.</p>

      <p>For further examples of negative sentences in Cherokee, see the
        discussion of the negative prefixes y- and n- in Section <a
          href="#I-A">I-A</a>.</p>
      <p><a name="IV-B">IV-B</a>. <u>Questions</u></p>
      <p>Questions which can be answered appropriately with yes or no
        may be formed in Cherokee by the use of the question suffixes
        -sgo and -ju; see <a href="#I-D">I-D</a> for examples.
        Questions with "or," such as "Is it a man or a woman?" may be
        formed with the suffixes -sgo and -ke; see <a href="#I-D">I-D</a>
        for a discussion of -ke.</p>
      <p>Questions which request information may be formed by the use of
        the interrogative pronouns and other question words. The
        interrogative pronouns are discussed in <a href="#III-A">III-A</a>.
        Other question words not discussed above are illustrated by
        (1-3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎭᏜ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎮᎦ? <br>

            </td>
          </tr>

            <td><br>

            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">what</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he's doing to it</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The suffix -na "what about" is also used to form questions. See
        <a href="#I-D">I-D</a> and <a href="#II-B">II-B</a> for
        examples. Questions can also be marked in Cherokee by intonation
        changes, when surprise is indicated, as in (4).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>

            </td>

            <td>"a man?"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>

      <p><a name="IV-C">IV-C</a>. <u>Coordination</u></p>
      <p>When sentences which can stand alone are joined together, they
        are said to be coordinate.This section is a brief description of
        the process of coordination in Cherokee. Sentences may be
        connected by the conjunction ạ²le (see <a href="#III-F">III-F</a>)
        which occurs in Cherokee primarily in formal speech styles; note
        (1).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎴ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎨᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᎧᏃᎩᎠ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man is speaking and a
              woman is singing." <br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³nị²ha <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²le</td>
            <td>ạ²ge²³hya <br>
            </td>
            <td>de²³kạ³no²gi³ʔa. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>man</td>
            <td>is speaking</td>
            <td>and</td>
            <td>woman</td>
            <td>is singing</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The suffix -hno is used much more often than ạ²le; this suffix
        is attached to the first word of all sentences contained within
        compound sentence, with the exception of the first; note (2-3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ, <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎨᏯᏃ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᎧᏃᎩᎠ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man is speaking and a
              woman is singing."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²sgạ²ya <br>
            </td>
            <td>gạ²wo³nị²ha, <br>
            </td>
            <td>ạ²ge²³hyạ³hno <br>
            </td>
            <td>de²³kạ³no²gi³ʔa. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>man</td>
            <td>is speaking</td>
            <td>woman and</td>
            <td>is singing</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(3)&nbsp; </td>

            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎨᏯᏃ <br>

              is singing, and boys are conversing."</td>

            </td>

            <td><br>

      <p>Coordinate sentences with "or" are formed by the suffix -le
        together with yi⁴gi, as in (8-9).</p>

      <p>Note that sentences like (10), with identical intransitive
        verbs, are ungrammatical in Cherokee; the corresponding sentence
        (11), with the first verb deleted, is acceptable.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(10) <br>
            </td>
            <td><font color="red">*wi²³li</font></td>
            <td><font color="red">du¹hno²gi³sv³</font></td>
            <td><font color="red">da²³mị³hno</font></td>
            <td><font color="red">du¹hno²gi³sv²³ʔi</font>.</td>
            <td><font color="red">"Bill sang and Tom sang."</font></td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">Bill</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">sang</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">Tom and</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">sang</font></td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <br>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(11) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏫᎵ <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏓᎻᏃ <br>
            </td>

      <p>Note that the verb form in (11) is plural. Similarly, sentences
        like (12), with "or," are ungrammatical; reduced sentences like
        (13) are required instead.</p>

      <p>In coordinate sentences with identical verbs, the first verb
        may not be deleted; sentences such as (22a) are thus
        grammatical, while (22b) is not.</p>

              potato." </td>

            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">da²³mị³hno</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">nu²³na</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">u¹du²lị³ha</font>.</td>
            <td><font color="red">"Bill wants bread and Tom a potato."</font></td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">Bill</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">bread</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">Tom and</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">potato</font></td>
            <td style="text-align: center;"><font color="red">wants</font></td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>(22b) cannot be understood as a coordinate sentence which has
        undergone deletion of an identical verb. Its only possible
        meaning is "Bill (is) bread, and Tom wants a potato."</p>
      <p>Sentences exhibiting identical verb deletion may also have the
        word order of (23).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
