      <p><a name="IV-D">IV-D</a>. <u>Subordination</u></p>
      <p>Subordinate clauses cannot stand alone; they occur only in
        compound sentences. A number of types of subordinate clauses,
        introduced by subordinate conjunctions, are noted in Section <a
          href="#III-F">III-F</a>. Some additional types of subordinate
        clauses are discussed briefly in this section.</p>
      <p><u>"if"-clauses </u></p>
      <p>Clauses translated by "if" in English are formed in Cherokee by
        adding the prefix y- (see Section <a href="#I-A">I-A</a>) to
        the verb, as in (1).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏃᏊ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏱᎦᏬᏂᎭ,<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">Ꮭ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏯᏆᏅᎳ.<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"If he is speaking now, I
              don't know about it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">no²³gwu</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yị²gạ³wo³²ni⁴ha,</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hla³</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yạ²gwanh³ta.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">now</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">if he's speaking</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">not</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">not I know</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Non-present tense "if"-clauses require y-, the Set B subject
        pronoun prefixes, and the non-progressive form of the verb stem
        followed by the -a suffix (see <a href="#I-C">I-C</a>); note
        (2-3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">Ꮭ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏯᏆᏛᎦᏁ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏒᎯ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏳᏬᏂᏌ.<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"If he spoke yesterday, I
              didn't hear about it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hla³</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yạ³kwtv²ga¹ne</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">sv²hi</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yu²³wo³²ni⁴sa.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">not</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">not I heard</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yesterday</td>

      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 351</p>
      <p>The subordinate clause may either precede the main clause, as
        in (1) and (3), or follow it, as in (2). </p>
      <p><u>"when"-clauses</u></p>
      <p>The verbs of "when"-clauses referring to past action take the
        Set B prefixes and the non-progressive past tense form of the
        stem followed by -a, as in (4)./p&gt;
        <table>
          <tbody>
            <tr>
              <td style="vertical-align: top;">(4) <br>
              </td>
              <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᏍᎬᎢ<br>
              </td>
              <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎤᎪᎭ.<br>
              </td>
              <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He was speaking when he
                saw it."</td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
              <td><br>
              </td>
              <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³ni²sgv²³ʔi <br>
              </td>
              <td style="text-align: center;">u¹go⁴ha.</td>
              <td><br>
              </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
              <td><br>
              </td>
              <td style="text-align: center;">he was speaking <br>
              </td>
              <td style="text-align: center;">when he saw it <br>
              </td>
            </tr>
          </tbody>
        </table>
      </p>
      <p>The verbs of "when"-clauses referring to future action take the
        Set A prefixes and the non-progressive stem followed by -vʔi, as
        in (5). When habitual action is referred to, the verb appears in
        the form described above for the past tense; note (6).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(5) <br>

            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I'll be there when he
              speaks."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">wị²ge²³do³²he³sdi</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³ni²sv⁴ʔi. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>

            </td>

            <td><br>

      <p><u>"before"-clauses "</u></p>
      <p>Before"-clauses are formed by adding the prefix n- to the verb
        and suffixing -na followed by -gwu, as in (7).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(7) <br>

            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He spoke before I got
              there."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">wị²nạ²giʔ³luh²jv²³nạ²gwu</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">u¹wo³ni²sv²³ʔi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">before I arrived there</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he spoke</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>"<u>after" -clauses</u></p>

        suffix -oʔi, as in (8).</p>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏂᎬᏁᎲᎢ.<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"I don't know how he is
              doing it."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hla³</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">yạ²gwanh³ta</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">nị²gv³ne²hv⁴ʔi.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">no</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">I don't know</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">how he's doing it</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p><a name="IV-E">IV-E</a>. <u>Possession</u></p>
      <p>It was noted in <a href="#II-C">II-C</a> that terms for body
        parts and kinship terms occur with pronoun prefixes, as in
        (1-2).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td>(1)</td>
            <td>a. <br>

            <td>"your hand"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>

            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏰᏂ <br>
            </td>
            <td>ụ²wo¹ye⁴ni</td>
            <td>"his hand"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>(2)</td>
            <td>a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎩᏙᏓ <br>
            </td>

            <td>"my father"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>

            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏙᏓ <br>

            <td>"your father"</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>

            </td>

            </td>
            <td>u²do²da</td>
            <td>"his father"</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>The forms of the possessive pronoun, discussed in <a
          href="#III-A">III-A</a>, are employed in possessive
        constructions involving nouns which refer to inanimate objects,
        animals, and detached body parts; note (3-5).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>

      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 353</p>
      <p><a name="IV-F">IV-F</a>. <u>Notes on word order in Cherokee</u></p>
      <p>In simple declarative sentences in Cherokee, the subject of the
        sentence ordinarily precedes the verb with its modifiers and
        objects. In addition, objects of verbs ordinarily precede the
        verb, resulting in subject-object-verb word order in sentences
        like (1a); the inversion of this order is possible, yielding the
        pattern object-verb-subject, as in (1b).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(1) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏪᏌ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᎭ.<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man sees a cat."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">we²sa</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹gowh²tị³ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">man</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">cat</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">sees</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">b. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏪᏌ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᎭ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ.<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A man sees a cat."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">we²³sa</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹gowh²tị³ha</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sgạ²ya.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">cat</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">sees</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">man</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Other word orders may occur. Negative sentences, for example,
        exhibit different patterns than declarative sentences; see the
        example sentences in <a href="#IV-A">IV-A</a>.</p>
      <p> <u>Word order within noun phrases.</u> Noun phrases consist
        of a pronoun or of a noun with or without modifiers; noun
        phrases function as subjects of sentences and as objects of
        verbs. They may also have other functions. They may serve, for
        example, as objects of prepositions and as adverbs of location.
        For the latter function, see the discussion of the locative
        suffix in <a href="#II-B">II-B</a>.</p>
      <p>Noun phrases may consist of a noun modified by an adjective, as
        in (2); note that the adjective precedes the noun.</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(2) </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎤᏍᏗ </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏧᏣ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏜᏱᎭ. </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"A little boy is crying."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ụ²sdi</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²chu⁴ja </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹dlo²hyị³ha. </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">little</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">boy</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">is crying</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Demonstratives like "this" and "that" occur at the beginning of
        noun phrases; numeral adjectives follow the demonstratives and
        precede other adjectives, as in (3).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(3) &lt;</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮎ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏔᎵ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏬᏂᎭ.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"The two men are speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>na³</td>
            <td>ạ²ni²³tạʔ³li</td>
            <td>ạ²ni²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td>a¹ni²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td>the</td>
            <td>two</td>
            <td>men</td>
            <td>are speaking</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Relative clauses may follow the nouns they modify as in (4a),
        or they may follow them as in (4b).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(4)</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">a. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᎠ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏔᎵ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎭᏂ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏥᏓᏂᏙᎦ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏬᏂᎭ.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"These two men who are
              standing here are speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ³hạ²ni</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">jị²da¹ni²³do¹ga</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹ni²wo³nị²ha. <br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>

          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>

            <td style="text-align: center;">these</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">two</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">men</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">here</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">who are standing <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">are speaking</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">b.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎯᎠ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎯᏂ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᏥᏓᏂᏙᎦ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏔᎵ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏂᏬᏂᎭ.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"These two men who are
              standing here are speaking."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">hị²ʔa</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ³hạ²ni</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">jị²da¹ni²³do¹ga</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ni²³tạʔ³li </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²ni²sgạ²ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">a¹ni²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">these</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">here</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">who are standing <br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">two</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">men</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">are speaking</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>Note that the only difference in word order between (4a) and
        (4b) is that the relative clause occupies the position
        immediately following the demonstrative and preceding the
        adjective in (4b); in (4a) it occurs in the final position
        within the noun phrase.</p>
      <p> <u>Word order within verb phrases.</u> Adverbs precede the
        verbs they modify, as in (5).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(5) </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᏓᏯ</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;"><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sda⁴ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td>"He's speaking loudly."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">loud</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he's speaking</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>An adverb may itself be modified by another adverb, as in (6) .</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 354</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>
          <tr>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">(6) <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎤᏙᎯᏳ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᏍᏓᏯ<br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
            </td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"He's really speaking
              loudly."</td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td>&nbsp;<br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ụ²do²hị²yu⁴</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ạ²sda⁴ya</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">ga²wo³nị²ha.</td>
            <td><br>
            </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
            <td><br>
            </td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">really</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">loud</td>
            <td style="text-align: center;">he is speaking</td>
          </tr>
        </tbody>
      </table>
      <p>As noted above, noun phrases which function as objects of verbs
        precede the verb; see (1).</p>
      <p><u>Some additional comments on Cherokee word order.</u>
        Sentences in Cherokee do not always exhibit a verb. Cherokee
        sentences may consist, for example, of two noun phrases in
        juxtaposition, as in (7), or of a noun phrase followed by an
        adjective, as in (8).</p>
      <table>
        <tbody>

            <td style="vertical-align: top; text-align: center;">ᎠᎩᏙᏓ.</td>
            <td style="vertical-align: top;">"That man is my father."</td>
          </tr>

      <p>See the discussion of "to be" in <a href="#I-F">I-F</a> for
        further details regarding sentences without overt verb forms.
        For further information regarding word order in general in
        Cherokee, see the illustrative sentences in the dictionary and
        the example sentences in preceding sections of the grammar
        outline.</p>

      <p>ᎠᏂᏔᎵ ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ ᎠᏂᏃᎭᎵᏙᎮ, ᎠᏫ ᏚᏂᏲᎮ. ᏌᏊᏃ ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᎦᎶᏇ ᎦᏁᎮ ᏐᎢᏅ Ꮭ. ᎾᏅ ᎦᎶᏇ ᎦᏁᎯ
        ᎤᏢᏈᏌᏘ ᎨᏎ, ᎠᎴ ᎪᎱᏍᏗ ᏳᏛᏁᎳ ᎠᏢᏈᏍᎨ. ᎠᎾᎢᏒᏅ ᎪᎱᏍᏗ ᏭᏂᎪᎮ ᏙᏧᏩᎢᏢ, ᎾᏅ ᎦᎶᏇ ᎦᏁᎯ
        ᎩᎳᏊ</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 355</p>
      <p>ᎢᏴᏓ ᏫᏚᏍᏓᏲᏞ ᎣᏍᏓᏊ ᏄᎦᏎᏍᏔᏅᎾ ᎢᏳᏍᏗ ᏓᏍᏓᏲᎯᎲ. ᎤᏙᎯᏳᏅ ᏭᏲᏞ ᎠᎴ ᎤᏂᎪᎮ ᎦᏅᎬ. ᎾᏅ
        ᎦᎶᏇ ᏂᎦᏁᎲᎾ "ᎠᏫ ᎡᏂᏲᎠ!" ᎤᏛᏁ. "ᏥᏲᎡᎦ" ᎤᏛᏁ Ꮎ ᎦᎶᏇ ᎦᏁᎯ, ᎠᏢᏈᏍᎬ. ᎤᎾᏁᎷᎩᏎ
        ᏙᏧᏩᎢᏢ ᏗᏜ, ᎾᎥᏂᎨᏅ ᏭᏂᎷᏣ ᎤᏂᎪᎮ ᏐᏈᎵ ᎦᏅ. "ᏐᏈᎵᎴ ᎢᎾᏓᎯᏏ" ᎤᏛᏁ Ꮎ ᎤᏲᏢ.
        "ᎭᏓᎯᏎᎦ" ᎠᎪᏎᎴ.</p>
      <p>Two men were hunting, looking for deer. One of the men had a
        gun and the other one didn't. The one with the gun was a
        braggart and when he did something he bragged about it. Walking
        along, they saw something moving around in the bushes, and the
        man with the gun shot at once toward the bushes without paying
        attention to what he was shooting at. And sure enough he shot it
        and they saw it fall to the ground. The one without the gun said
        "we shot a deer." "<u>I</u> shot it," said the one with the gun,
        bragging. They raced toward the bushes and when they got closer
        they saw a horse lying there. "We killed someone's horse," said
        the one who shot it. "<u>You</u> killed it," he was told.</p>
      <p>&nbsp;</p>
      <hr>
      <hr>
      <p><sup>1</sup><a name="footnote1"></a> We would like to thank
        Bill Cook for discussing various aspects of Cherokee with us,
        and for pointing out a number of facts regarding the future
        tense prefix and the suffix system of the verb. We would also
        like to thank Willard Walker for generously making available to
        us many of his unpublished materials on Cherokee. We have also
        profited from reading Reyburn's study of Cherokee morphology
        ("Cherokee Verb Morphology I, II," <i>International Journal of
          American Linguistics</i>, 19: 172-180, 259-73, and "Cherokee
        Verb Morphology III," <i>International Journal of American
          Linguistics</i>, 20:44-64).</p>
      <p><sup>2</sup><a name="footnote2"></a> Final vowels are more
        often retained at the end of sentences or utterances than
        elsewhere, apparently indicating that they function as carriers
        of the final part of intonation contours. The retention or
        dropping of final vowels also seems to mark the extent of
        formality of various speech styles; many more final vowels are
        retained in oratorical style than in casual spoken style, for
        example.</p>
      <p><sup>*i</sup><a name="footnote*i"> The original has <i>(22-23)</i>
          while the actual examples are numbered (23) and (24). -CK</a></p>
      <p><a name="footnote*i"><sup>*ii</sup></a><a name="footnote*ii">
          The original refers to <i>non-motion verbs</i>; however,
          example 113 ("he's walking") is clearly a motion verb. -TLW</a></p>
    </summary>
